our data HBESSY 2010L

Kossmann et al.

Figure 55: βm as a function of photon energy combined with a fit of a transition function:
For high photon energies the molecular angular distribution parameter βm turns into -1 which
describes an anisotropic distribution. Low energy data of Kossmann et al. [74] can be continued
with our data.

The transition effect presented in section 6.2 can be understood in the following picture: When
the electron de Broglie wavelength resolves the internuclear distance, the probability of the ion-
ization process is increased for a 90° orientation of the molecular axis to the polarization vector.
This results in a transition from random orientation properties (for an outgoing wavelength
at lower photon energies, which not resolves the molecule) to oriented properties because one
orientation is preferred. Therefore, the dominant double photoionization excitation process has
to be Σ → Π as discussed in section 2.5.5, since after the transition the βm value is close to
-1 (starting at a value of -0.53 and leading to -0.96). The mean photon energy position of the
βm transition is at 451 eV but with a large width of about 340 eV. At this energy position, the
transition of the relative cross section has already taken place (see figure 53), indicating that
the influence of a βm change to the phase of the relative cross section oscillations seems to be
very strong. Comparing our anisotropy parameter values with results of Kossmann et al. [74]
shows a good compatibility and continuation (see figure 55). In order to give a more accessible

72



presentation of the measured data, figure 57 compares both angular distributions, before (blue)
and after (red) the transition40 in a polar plot.

6.5 Branching Ratio

Besides the absolute analysis of structures in spectra, relative ratios are always very interesting.
They are independent of many measurement parameters over a wide range apart of statistics,
transmission and saturation effects. A change of gas pressure, light intensity or the measurement
time are reflected directly in the counting rate of the detectors, i.e. the absolute values. For
example, the relative proportions of two lines of a spectrum are independent thereof. An
important relation in the hydrogen molecule ion spectra is the ratio between H+- to H2+-peak
intensities as a function of photon energy.
For photon energies below 124 eV this ratio has been published with good energy resolution by
Chung et al. [22]. Our measurements extend this energy range up to 530 eV again using the ion
tof with either a constant field at the pusher and extractor (at PETRA III, cyan dots in figure
58) or in pulsed mode (at BESSY II, blue dots in figure 58). In BESSY II data, the second bunch
of the pulsed spectra (shown in figure 35) is taken to have almost the same conditions as in the
Chung et al. measurements [22]. The first bunch in the ion spectra contains stationary ions.
They have accumulated over several bunches since the pusher was not reset with each bunch

74



marker41. The BESSY II branching ratios almost seamlessly extend the ratios from Chung et al.
[22]. Although the slope of the ratios taken at PETRA III fits well to the BESSY II ratios, the
absolute values are smaller. The discrepancy between the BESSY II and the PETRA III data
is caused by the different pulse distances in combination with dead time of the TDCs which is
typically 20 ns (30 ns maximum). In the setup at P04 from S. Deinert at al. [34] the ion flight
tube has a length of 3 cm and the distance between the bunches was 192 ns. This leads to a
poorer time resolution of the Coulomb explosion process which is spread into about 50 ns ion
time-of-flight (compared to 150 ns at the BESSY II experiment). Therefore, a larger number of
the incoming H+ ions are not detected resulting in a smaller branching ratio. By assuming a
loss ≈ 30% of H+ ions and performing a rescaling of the measured branching ratio, one derives
the ’transmission corrected’ curve (darker cyan) in figure 58 that fits well to the BESSY II data.
At about 250 eV that branching ratio has a minimum and then it seems to grow again for rising
photon energy. An overview of an equally interesting relationship, comparing the intensity of
1s mainline to the satellites of electron time-of-flight spectra, is given in appendix 8.2.3.

6.6 High photon energies

At photon energies higher than the oxygen K-edge of the H2O molecule [92], the cross section
oscillation observation becomes much more difficult. Here, the residual gas measurements turn
out to be as important as the ones with H2 gas in the chamber. The majority counts measured
in the H+ region are caused by the ionization of the residual gas, mostly water42. This is shown
in figure 59 for a gas mixture of helium and molecular hydrogen at 1000 eV photon energy
recorded with the detector setup of S. Deinert [34] at the PETRA III P04 beamline [121]. The
H+ feature of the H2 molecule in the center of the presentation, surrounded by the He+ and
the He2+/H2 peak, is located on top of a large residual gas feature. Nevertheless, the difference
spectrum (gas minus residual) shows the Coulomb explosion in an impressive way for that high
photon energy.

region the strength and influence of residual gas spectra is too strong to be removed from the

that the main statement here is about the phase and the question whether the oscillation of
the random target follows either the magenta or the blue sine curve in figure 60. This can be
decided clearly: After the transition the phase of the random sample remains constant, i.e. no
difference of the random and oriented sample is observed. Having only circular polarization
available at our P04 beamtimes, it was impossible to determine the βm value. Considering that
the randomly distributed sample oscillation stays on the curve of the oriented molecule (blue
curve in figure 60), it is assumed that the βm value stays close to -1 at this high photon energies.

77



7 Conclusion and outlook

The present study is divided into two main parts: Sequential two-photon double ionization in
noble gases (helium, neon, argon and krypton) and cross section oscillations in the hydrogen
molecule. Electron and ion time-of-flight spectroscopy was performed to investigate atomic and
molecular ionization properties. While our campaigns for ion fragment analysis were executed at
the synchrotron sources BESSY II (U125/2, UE56/1-PGM-b, UE56/2 PGM1: 28 - 650 eV) and
PETRA III (P04: 265 - 1200 eV) over a large photon energy range, the FEL light source FLASH
(BL2, BL3: 38 - 91 eV) was utilized for investigations of non-linear multi-photon processes in
noble gases.
Up to 19 independently working electron time-of-flight detectors in a spherical chamber were
used for photoelectron angular distribution analysis of the sequential two-photon ionization of
rare gases. Starting with an experimental proof of theoretically predicted [48] β4 effects in the
first ionization step of neon, argon and krypton, this thesis presents the importance of studying
these processes as a function of photon density. The evaluation of the β2,4 values as a function
of FLASH pulse energy was possible because of the intensity fluctuations due to the statistical
nature of the SASE process. Since a huge amount of data has been generated at our FLASH
experiments (45 terabytes), a detailed description of the data analysis is accomplished with a
resulting CUDA-based evaluation software. This tool is optimized for parallel fitting of a large
amount of electron time-of-flight spectra created by single FEL shots. It provides a visualization
tool to inspect a large number of recorded data in a quick and intuitive way. As a further result,
the second step β2,4 parameters are compared to calculations of Fritzsche et al. [48, 49, 50] and
A. Kheifets [69] with good agreement. The strong deviation in both ionization steps from the
expected 1s angular distribution in helium at 61 eV is explained by a nearby resonance with an
overlap with the FLASH energy distribution. Follow-up experiments as well as efforts towards
more detailed theoretical simulations are necessary for a better understanding of the presented
sequential double ionization and of multi-photon ionization processes induced by high-intensity
FEL light sources. To give a more detailed analysis of the influence of the resonance on the
angular distribution an energy scan could be performed in the region of interest starting on top
of the resonance to an energy position without an overlap. This could consists of 5 energies at
FLASH (lower and higher energy with and without overlap and on resonance) or even with a
higher energy resolution at FERMI, where the design of a seeded FEL gives the advantage of
narrow photon energy bandwidth and enhanced wavelength tuning capabilities. Investigations
of double ionization processes at higher photon energies will be provided by the European XFEL
where the data flood will even increase and specially adjusted evaluation software will be needed
as well.
As a final result of the first step β4 behavior of noble gases and the helium resonance, it can
be concluded that a strict two-step model does not apply for the sequential two-photon double

78



ionization in rare gases. Both ionization steps are coherently coupled.
The so-called Cohen Fano [23] oscillations in the relative cross section of the hydrogen molecule
are investigated over a large photon energy range covering almost two oscillation periods (28 -
1200 eV). It is an additional coherence effect in the photoionization. These Young type interfer-
ences are caused by the inversion symmetric character of a homonuclear molecule and by the
resulting electronic states being a coherent superposition of both atomic sites. It was possible
to distinguish whether the electrons are emitted from a target that is oriented randomly or from
a fixed-in-space target. This should give rise to a phase shift of 90° between both oscillations
which was observed at low photon energies. However, the βm induced transition from random to
oriented target properties takes place when the de Broglie wavelength of the outgoing electron
resolves the internuclear distance (transition from βm = −0.53 and leading to βm = −0.96). An
orientation of the molecular axis to the photon polarization vector is preferred for the photoion-
ization process and the dominant excitation is Σ → Π (βm = −1). This appears without any
external tuning. In addition, the important branching ratio between H+- to H2+-peak intensities
in the hydrogen molecule ion spectra as a function of photon energy was analyzed. This ratio
was published by Chung et al. for photon energies below 124 eV [22] and our measurements
extend the energy range up to 530eV. The experimental setup consists of several variants of
an ion time-of-flight spectrometer (position sensitive, pure time-of-flight spectroscopy, different
drift tube lengths) with the option of a coincidence mode with an electron time-of-flight detector
based on the Wiley McLaren setup [126]. The described phase shift is also observed in the inner
shell photoionization of the nitrogen molecule [80, 112, 133] also tunable by the photoelectron’s
de Broglie wavelength. As a result, the transition can be put in a more general context and
opens a wide field of future projects. The high photon flux at PETRA III P04 beamline will
be combined with linear light polarization in the very future. This will allow to analyze the βm
parameter up to high photon energies in H2, N2 and also for other transition candidates such as
O2. In addition to the existing great setup of Deinert et al. [34] a new detector setup (prototype
status at the moment) of A. Meißner et al. could also be used for the analysis of the relative
cross section and the ionic fragment angular distribution. This position sensitive detector will
especially solve any multi-hit problems. Combined with position information and the option of
electron coincidences a lot of interesting future projects could be initiated. Regardless of the
experimental point of view, the theoretical simulation of the transition effect can be performed
containing the change of βm and covering the large energy range explored in this thesis.
The general conclusion of the present study is how much quantum information concerning
coherence effects is carried in angular distributions.

79

8.2 Additional information to the H2 Data

8.2.1 More details and comparison of H2 data

Figure 63 shows an overview of the data compilation with error bars, additional random sample
data of Stolterfoth et al. [113] and calculations of Fojon et al. [45]. Since the BESSY II run of
2005 was optimized for the electron-ion-coincidence mode, the recorded ion spectra (see figure
62) have not the same quality as the ones shown in section 4.2.1. However, the ion spectra
analysis (green dots) is in surprisingly good agreement with the coincidence results. In order to
extend the energy range of randomly distributed targets with the BESSY II data of 2010, the
normalization of H+-peaks are performed by

the recorded spectra. The time-of-flight spectra are normalized to the area of the sharp central
peak for a better comparison.

83



8.2.3 Electron branching ratio

An interesting relationship in the electron spectrum is the intensity of the main 1s line compared
to the intensity of the photoelectron satellites [78, 125]. Figure 65 shows a time-of-flight electron
spectrum that contains the 1s mainline and the satellites taken at 80 eV photon energy. The
energy dependent branching ratio of the respective intensities is shown in 66. This branching
ratio could separate dominant excitations of different ionic final states. From our measured
data which cover only a smaller energy region it cannot concluded whether or not there is an
oscillation or a similar behavior in the 1s/satellite ratio such as the observed H+/H+2 ions ratio.
This may be part of future investigations.

8.4 Matrix evaluation of sequential ionization processes

8.4.1 First step calibration consistency

If a second ionization step occurs only a portion of the area of the first step signal is correlated to
the double ionization as presented in figure 34 and discussed in section 4.1.6. Without a second
step occurring, the first step is completely described with a β2 distribution. The low intensity
shots are used to calibrate on a known single ionization β2 single ionization value in order to
analyze the first step β4 behavior. This allows to inspect the spectra with higher FLASH pulse
energy as described in section 4.1.3. For the recorded GMD distributions no discernible second
step occurs at the lower 10%-35% of the spectra. These spectra are used for the presented
results in section 5.1. As consistency check, figure 68 shows the β4 behavior for a calibration
with the 10% -60% weakest shots. This consistency analysis shows even for 60% calibration a

Acknowledgements

The scientific passion of Professor Uwe Becker is this thesis’ motivation. He was always inter-
ested in giving me the possibility and time needed for a deeper and deeper understanding of
quantum physics - and politics, biology, history, psychology, industry, economy, art, ... which
we were discussing at our very special group meetings over the years. In the familiar working
environment, everything was allowed except his always quoted

’...denn sie wissen nicht, was sie tun’.

German title46 of James Dean’s motion capture ’Rebel Without a Cause’.

The years in Uwe’s group formed my scientific character in such a special way that I will always
be thankful for. Therefore, I want to thank all members of the AG Becker during my time,
namely Sanja Korica, Markus Braune, Toralf Lischke, André Meißner and Max Stammer.
When Uwe was taken from us far too early, I just started preparing this thesis and was over-
whelmed by the help I received by cooperation partners and former members of the work
group. Jens Viefhaus was instantaneously offering to be a reviewer in association with Pro-
fessor Thomas Möller, who generously helped bringing this thesis to completion. Professor
Christian Thomsen, Professor Arno Ehresmann and André Knie also directly offered their sup-
port in order to solve any arising problems.
Additionally, I want to thank Burkhard Langer, Markus Braune, Cemil Yigit and Toralf Lischke
for their proofreeding. Especially Burkhard was a tremendous help in the crucial period.
Starting my scientific career in theoretical physics led by Ermin Malic in the group of Professor
Andreas Knorr [31, 55, 83], I ’changed sides’ and was introduced to the experimental process by
Markus Braune, Toralf Lischke, André Meißner, Burkhard Langer, Jens Viefhaus, André Knie
and Rainer Hentges.
Fruitful discussions about the data evaluation and interpretation were hold with Markus Braune,
Jens Viefhaus, Burkhard Langer, Markus Ilchen, Professor Nikolay Kabachnik, Professor Alexei
N. Grum-Grzhimailo and Ralph Püttner.
I want to emphasize how thankful I am for the support from and guidance of Markus Braune.
He was always available for discussions, even after he moved to DESY. He was and is an
indispensable help and motivation.
The FLASH experiments were performed before I was a member of AG Becker. Therefore, I
want to thank everyone who was involved in the data recording.
Philipp ’Max’ Stammer’s endless desire in studying physics and helping our group was a special
help at exhausting beamtimes and on the calibration of the detectors.

46Key statement freely translated: ’...since they don’t know what they do’

104



The setup of Sascha Deinert combined with the outstanding support of the P04 crew (Jens
Viefhaus, Leif Glaser, Sascha Deinert, Frank Scholz, Joern Seltmann and Jens Buck) make
the H2 data extension up to 1200eV possible. This was obviously a heavy improvement of this
thesis’ major statement. In addition, Philipp Schmidt assisted during a P04 beamtime in a very
helpful way.
My best friend Cemil Yigit was an incredible help as my classmate when studying physics and
during my PhD time. Without him, a lot of arising scientific, programming and visualization
problems would have been remained unsolved significantly longer. Therefore, I’m more than
thankful.
I can’t find the right words to give my parents, Christine and Rainer Hartmann, the credit they
deserve for supporting me my whole life.
Finally, I guess each scientist would agree that having a fellow who covers your back during
tough times as a doctoral student is a priceless precious. I’m just happy to call Eva Rettinger
my fiancee.
